<div id="review_2054343560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2054343560" />
    <a title="G33z3r" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7136749-g33z3r"><img alt="G33z3r" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402613612p2/7136749.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2054343560?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="G33z3r" class="user" itemprop="url" name="G33z3r" href="/user/show/7136749-g33z3r">G33z3r</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/35628476-the-coming-of-conan-the-cimmerian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=sword-sorcery">sword-sorcery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=sff-2017-reading-challenge">sff-2017-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=sff-2017-discussion-challenge">sff-2017-discussion-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=sff-2017-shorts-challenge">sff-2017-shorts-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7136749-g33z3r?shelf=sfhf-group-discussion">sfhf-group-discussion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2054343560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1424835989129416039">A collection that introduces the original Robert E Howard Conan, in all his muscled, fighting, warring, wenching, pirating, murdering, thieving, raping, racist glory.<br/><br/><br/><b>The Phoenix on the Sword</b><br/>It's interesting that the first Conan story Howard published placed him as King of Aquilonia (though a former Barbarian), since our popular image of him is the peripatetic thief, rogue & reaver. This isn't the only King Conan story in the collection, either.<br/><br/>The beginning of this particular story, in which Kin</span>
  <span id="freeText1424835989129416039" style="display:none">A collection that introduces the original Robert E Howard Conan, in all his muscled, fighting, warring, wenching, pirating, murdering, thieving, raping, racist glory.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Phoenix on the Sword</b><br />It's interesting that the first Conan story Howard published placed him as King of Aquilonia (though a former Barbarian), since our popular image of him is the peripatetic thief, rogue &amp; reaver. This isn't the only King Conan story in the collection, either.<br /><br />The beginning of this particular story, in which King Conan muses that civilization has caused him to lose his barbaric edge, put me in mind of Tennyson's brooding Ulysses.<br /><br />This story also introduces the Stygian wizard Thoth Amon and the serpent-god Set. There's intrigue afoot in the palace, and an assassination is planned.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Frost-Giant's Daughter</b><br />A short story meant to have a mysterious tone. In the aftermath of a great battle on the snows, a tired &amp; wounded Conan, lone survivor, sees a lovely woman. He chases after her teasing form... "Conan the Wannabe Rapist". Well, like the title says, she's got a mean daddy.<br /><br />At the end, it's a "was that real or was that a delusion/dream" type story, with a small kick at the end. I didn't think it quite pulled off the intended enigma, possibly because the little twist at the end is too close on the reveal.<br /><br /><br /><b>The God in the Bowl</b><br />This is an interesting mixed-genre story, a sort of Conan meets Columbo. A detective story in which Conan sees mostly out of place. Conan is caught red handed with a dead body in a sort of locked-room murder mystery. Whodunit? Conan says he didn't — he's just robbing the place. (He would have if he had to, but he didn't.) The magistrate of the city guard isn't so sure....<br /><br />The ending is little telegraphed. Note how quick the fight is compared to Phoenix and the Sword.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Tower of the Elephant</b><br />This is one of the classic Conan stories. It divides into two sections.<br /><br />In part 1, Conan is hanging in a tavern listening to tales of a fabulous gem in the local Tower of the Elephant. The teller asserts its thief-proof, and Conan disagrees.<br /><br />The tavern-goers are rather rude to poor Conan, leading to Howard's comment, "Civilized men are more discourteous than savages because they know they can be impolite without having their skulls split." Conan corrects them. This correction is lethal in nature.<br /><br />Impulsively, Conan decides to go rob the tower.<br /><br />Part 2 is the tower caper. Conan runs into a fellow thief in the surrounding garden, and they team up. Taurus makes quite a contrast, as he has planned all the details of his burglary, compared to Conan doing it on a tavern dare.<br /><br />I liked the general story, although it's familiar fantasy fare in the current day: break into dungeon guarded by traps &amp; monsters, steal treasure.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The fact that the careful-planning Taurus ends up dead and Conan survives is, I assume, Howard's way of again suggesting intellect is no match for a barbarian's reflexes. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><b>The Scarlet Citadel</b><br />A second story featuring Conan the King of Aquilonia (as with The Phoenix on the Sword). <br /><br />Conan is betrayed by a couple of fellow kings, notably Pelias, and his army lured into a trap; Conan tossed in a wizard's dungeon (because killing him would be.... wrong?) . So we have an prison-break story (aided by a fellow inmate who's helpfully a sorcerer) followed by a vengeance story, ending with a big battle as Conan wants his kingdom back.<br /><br />Along the way we learn Conan was a wise King: Fair justice, no corruption, low taxes. And the first &amp; only helpful sorceror in the story.<br /><br />Decent enough story. How come no one ever just kills a defeated hero? (At least Conan isn't Batman; when Conan beats an enemy, the enemy gets dead &amp; stays dead.)<br /><br /><br /><b>Queen of the Black Coast</b><br />A cool pirate story in multiple chapters.<br /><br />Conan hijacks a whole merchant ship to escape the local city guards, real quick starter. Then the ship is attacked by Pirates: the infamous <i>Tigress</i> commanded by the white goddess Bêlit, "Queen of the Black Coast". It's love at first sight. Conan can't bring himself to stick an arrow in her, and she calls off her pirates and takes him for her lover. (She takes the merchant's cargo, too; she's not that besotted!) The chapter is title "Conan Joins the Pirates", but it's not like he had a choice.<br /><br />We then have a sea serpent (It's always snakes with this guy.) Giant bats, drugged plant life, treasure, and finally tragedy.<br /><br /><br /><b>Black Colossus</b><br />A story in which Conan doesn't appear until the 2nd half.<br /><br />A thief cracks open an ancient pyramid and releases an ancient evil wizard. (Surprise! He had a giant snake!) Another example of an "intellectual" master-thief getting killed, by the way. Spontaneous Barbarians rule.)<br />The ancient wizard has a thing for a lovely Princess, and makes it known he's coming for her (with an army he assembled.) She seeks divine aid and Mitra sends her to Conan. Conan gets to lead the Princesses army against the Wizard's army. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As the armies clash, the Wizard manages to snatch the Princess, so Conan has to go chase him down. It's one of the anti-climatic endings, where Conan and the Wizard face off <i>mano-a-mano</i>, but Conan tosses his sword through the Wiz while the Wiz is still monologing. At least this Wiz went with a Giant Scorpion instead of a snake; alas, the Scorpion doesn't get to do much, thanks to the aforementioned sword impalement. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />I'm not sure where the title comes from.<br /><br />The story has some nice description of arid lands crossed by dry river washes, something Howard was likely familiar with in central Texas.<br /><br /><br /><b>Iron Shadows in the Moon </b><br />This is really the first story in the collection that left me bored.<br /><br />Conan rescues escaped girl from would-be rapist (oh, <i>now</i> he cares about rape!) She turns out to be a deposed Princess. He's fleeing from a lost battle. They go into hiding together. <br /><br />There's something evil lurking in the grass!<br /><br />Then, Pirates! Conan challenges the Captain &amp; kills him, asserting he's now Captain by right of combat. The pirates take him prisoner while they argue some abstruse legal pint as to whether Conan has standing to challenge. While waiting for the supreme court to rule, the deposed Princess rescues Conan (the great Barbarian rescued by a girl? ew!) <br /><br />Back to the evil lurking in the grass: Conan kills King Kong. The pirates beg him to be their Captain. <br /><br />I dunno, this all seemed very by-the-numbers barbarian fiction to me: Rescue the girl, kill the monster, be careful not to get that mixed up.<br /><br /><br /><b>Xuthal of the Dusk </b><br />I liked this story. There's something cool about a haunted city in the desert. Wasn't that the dead guy we saw before?<br /><br />We have no idea where comely wench Natala comes from, she's just clinging to Conan on their death march through the desert at the start of the story. They're saved from dying of thirst when they encounter a mysterious, seemingly empty city in the middle of nowhere. But the city isn't empty, it's inhabitants are just resting....<br /><br />I liked the mysterious haunted city idea, even though the story doesn't keep the mystery very long.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Pool of the Black One</b><br />A privateer ship is surprised when Conan just climbs over the gunwale &amp; boards, offering his services as sailor. Howard has a good foreshadowing line in Conan's introductory exchange with Capt. Zaporavo: "He hesitated, and doing so, lost his ship, his command, his girl, and his life. But of course he could not see into the future."<br /><br />It soon becomes a pirate ship under Conan's command. It's nice to see that even Conan knows when to cut &amp; run, as this time he doesn't stand and fight.<br /><br />This is, by the way, the 4th story in a row in which Conan ends up with a surviving female companion,... none of who seem to ever show up again. (I guess I could say the same about his pirate ships. :) <br /><br /><br /><b>Rogues in the House</b><br />Conan is in jail. What crime against civilization Conan committed to get tossed in jail? Apparently he killed a police informer. Howard is good at joining Conan <i>in medias res</i>.<br /><br />A nobleman, Murilo, wants rival priest, Nabonidus, dead, and arranges Conan's jailbreak in exchange for an assassination. It's worth noting the nobleman, Murilo, is just as corrupt. Murilo's only virtue is having the foresight to hire Conan.<br /><br /> Murilo thinks (wrongly) that the jailbreak has failed, and so both he and Cona are in Nabonidus together. The priest is a bit of technologist instead of a magician. He's invented the periscope, a gas trap, and a variety of other infernal devices to protect himself. <br /><br />I expected the ending <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[in which both Murilo &amp; Nabonidus ended up dead, both examples of the corrupting influence of civilization; but it seems Howard disagrees. I also wondered it Conan would reach an accommodation with Thak, the semi-evolved ape Nabonidus had enslaved. You'd think if Howard is in favor of the unspoiled savage as is claimed, Thak would be a purer savage than Conan, or at least his equal. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Vale of Lost Women</b><br />This is the only story in this collection written entirely in a PoV other than Conan. (It's all Livia's PoV.) It seems Howard enjoys using another PoV at the start so we get our first look at Conan as others see him, with some feigned ambiguity as to who the mysterious big guy is.<br /><br />Conan is at some sort of parlay with another leader, Bamulas, though the purpose of the parlay isn't revealed. Bamulas has taken a lovely white woman captive. She begs Conan to rescue her, promising her own body as reward. (Conan scoffs, "women are cheap as plantains.")<br /><br />The racism is explicit here: It seems even Conan is "not such a dog as to leave a white woman in the clutches of a black man." The blacks are casually slaughtered (breaking Conan's parley oath, BTW) for no better reason than Conan wants Livia for himself. Livia then decides to renege on her sex-for-rescue deal, and runs from frying pan into fire.<br /><br />I noticed this is the first real <i>short</i> story since; most of the stories in this collection have been novelette length (a distinction that didn't exist in Howard's day.)<br /><br />Is Conan a King in this story, or just leader of band of warriors? I wouldn't easily fit this into the Conan timeline. He could be king, pirate, general, or almost any leader.<br /><br />Anyway, the story itself is pretty standard: Conan rescues woman... twice.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Devil in Iron</b><br />The beginning, a lost fisherman stumbles into the resting place of some ancient evil and unintentionally releases it by taking a magic sword, very much like the start of "Black Colossus". <br /><br />Conan's enemy, Jehungir Agha, comes up with a really elaborate plan to trap Conan alone – by luring him to a taboo island by dangling a comely slave-wench, Octavia, as bait. It seems overly complex, not to mention farfetched. So of course, it works. Except for the aforementioned ancient evil that's not on the island.<br /><br />Hence, as in ""Iron Shadows of the Moon", Conan's enemies are killed by an evil monster, and it's left to Conan to kill the Iron Devil.<br /><br />Conan takes the unwilling woman (the bait) with a fiery kiss that is apparently sufficient to seduce her. (presumably to be discarded before the next story.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1424835989129416039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Nathan Wilder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92311150-nathan-wilder"><img alt="Nathan Wilder" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3536251246?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan Wilder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan Wilder" href="/user/show/92311150-nathan-wilder">Nathan Wilder</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3536251246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9133250640984588097">I don't like fantasy much but Conan hits all the right buttons for someone raised immediately post 80's action movie. <br/><br/>Conan is a man's man, built like a (panther? some type of big cat?) and goes on a variety of adventures which may or may not be linked. The concept is usually the same- Conan will enter into some supernatural situation or event, there will be people afterward who doubt him and then finally it will end with everyone realizing he was right all along. Similar pacing to H.P. Lovecraf</span>
  <span id="freeText9133250640984588097" style="display:none">I don't like fantasy much but Conan hits all the right buttons for someone raised immediately post 80's action movie. <br /><br />Conan is a man's man, built like a (panther? some type of big cat?) and goes on a variety of adventures which may or may not be linked. The concept is usually the same- Conan will enter into some supernatural situation or event, there will be people afterward who doubt him and then finally it will end with everyone realizing he was right all along. Similar pacing to H.P. Lovecraft and the shared universe really does have some uncanny moments. <br /><br />All in all a fantastic series that I can't wait to read more of.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9133250640984588097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_270954933" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270954933" />
    <a title="Stefan Yates" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7590340-stefan-yates"><img alt="Stefan Yates" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328116275p2/7590340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/270954933?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stefan Yates" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stefan Yates" href="/user/show/7590340-stefan-yates">Stefan Yates</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7590340-stefan-yates?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer270954933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16436802054452585707">Overall - I really enjoyed this first volume of the collected short stories of Robert E. Howard and his magnificent creation; Conan the Barbarian. There was not a story in the collection that I was bored with or disliked and I would heartily encourage those who enjoy the fantasy genre to delve into these stories and explore the roots of what started the genre that we enjoy today. The only faults that I have with Howard’s writing are the prevalent sexist and less prevalent racist attitudes that a</span>
  <span id="freeText16436802054452585707" style="display:none">Overall - I really enjoyed this first volume of the collected short stories of Robert E. Howard and his magnificent creation; Conan the Barbarian. There was not a story in the collection that I was bored with or disliked and I would heartily encourage those who enjoy the fantasy genre to delve into these stories and explore the roots of what started the genre that we enjoy today. The only faults that I have with Howard’s writing are the prevalent sexist and less prevalent racist attitudes that appear throughout these tales. I did find that I was able to overlook these flaws as a product of the attitudes of the general populace at the time of the stories origin and therefore could enjoy the works for their literary merit and the quality of the entertainment that I received from them even though I personally would have found these same ideas fairly offensive at times if present in modern day literature.<br /><br />Cimmeria – This is a one page poem that sets the tone for the book very nicely. It is very descriptive in giving color and setting for the stories to come.<br /><br />The Phoenix on the Sword – Excellent story to start the book. Set later in Conan’s life after he is King of Aquilonia, this story is about a plot to assassinate the great King Conan. The setup and pacing are very nicely done and there is an excellent battle scene as well.<br /><br />The Frost-Giant’s Daughter – Another fine tale, if the rest of the book keeps up at this pace, I’m in for a treat indeed. After a fierce battle in the arctic North, Conan meets a woman who he is driven to follow seemingly to his death. Howard’s prose and descriptive narrative are fantastic as the Frost Giant’s Daughter leads our hero through the frozen wastes.<br /><br />The God in the Bowl – Not quite as good as the previous two. Conan is caught while attempting a robbery and falsely accused of murdering a man who lies dead in the building. Most of the story revolves around the investigation of the murder. The end is decent though , and I still liked it overall.<br /><br />The Tower of the Elephant – The most action packed story in the book so far. Conan enters the mysterious and perilous Tower of the Elephant to steal a powerful gem from a sorcerer. There are many unexpected traps surrounding the tower and the action is pretty much non-stop.<br /><br />The Scarlet Citadel – King Conan leads his army into battle to assist a fellow Lord. Conan however is duped and taken prisoner after a massive battle. This one started out a bit slower than most of the others, but maintained my interest level none-the-less. There are some good dungeon crawling scenes and a fantastic final battle that makes this story really rock!!<br /><br />Queen of the Black Coast – Conan joins some sea-farers while being chased by local authorities in order to escape. I liked the change of pace in this one as it is a sea-faring adventure as opposed to the land-based battles of the previous ones. The crew finds a mysterious river and decides to travel up it to find the treasures that legend says are hidden somewhere up its poisoned waters. <br /><br />Black Colossus - This entry in the collection puts Conan at the head of an army. The story starts out a bit dry, but things pick up as it goes along and the battle scenes are quite entertaining.<br /><br />Iron Shadows in the Moon – Conan and a freed slave girl journey to a supposedly uninhabited island where they find a mysterious temple and a band of pirates awaiting them. Along with these perils is a beast that might be more than a match for Conan. <br /><br />Xuthal of the Dusk – Conan and his female companion wander through the desert to find a strange city. They meet a beautiful woman who rules the city and desires Conan. Foul deeds cause the disappearance of Conan’s woman and he must search the dark recesses of the city to find her. Howard creates a couple of very eerie and spooky scenes in this one that have a very good horror novel feel to them. It is a good example of the range that he has in his creative process.<br /><br />The Pool of the Black One – This is a rollicking action-packed adventure-filled good time. Another that begins on the sea, Conan is taken aboard as a cast-away and the ship travels to a mysterious island that the crew ventures to in order to explore. Mysterious things are found there and the battle scene and finale are among the best in the book.<br /><br />Rogues in the House – This is a slower paced story in which Conan is hired by a noble-man to assassinate a priest. Conan is sprung from prison by the young noble and in return must break into the fortress-like home of the priest and kill him. An interesting side note for those that enjoy the Conan films is that in this story we see the character and a few of the aspects of the scene with the mirror monster in Conan the Destroyer.<br /><br />The Vale of Lost Women – In another change of pace, Conan is the war chief of a band of black jungle-type warriors. This one started off a bit slow, but the pace picked up and I enjoyed it quite a bit. My only problems with this one are the racist attitudes displayed in a couple of points in the story by Howard. Of course, in its day, I doubt anyone would have taken much offense at all.<br /><br />The Devil in Iron – A mysterious being has risen from the grave and resurrected a ruined city on a deserted island. Conan must find the woman who is the current object of his desires and escape from this cursed place with both of their lives intact. This was a good story from start to finish and a fitting way to end the collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16436802054452585707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_540327973" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/540327973" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/540327973?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer540327973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10668857066776781265">This omnibus-thingy collects thirteen of Howard's original Conan stories as well as some cool miscellanea like poems, maps, rough drafts of a couple of the stories, etc. Okay, I came into this expecting (and desiring) pure pulpy cheesiness, and I definitely got some of that, but I was really struck with how literary and modern-feeling Howard's prose is. Honestly, the language in these stories really felt like it hadn't dated at all, even though most of these stories were written in the early 193</span>
  <span id="freeText10668857066776781265" style="display:none">This omnibus-thingy collects thirteen of Howard's original Conan stories as well as some cool miscellanea like poems, maps, rough drafts of a couple of the stories, etc. Okay, I came into this expecting (and desiring) pure pulpy cheesiness, and I definitely got some of that, but I was really struck with how literary and modern-feeling Howard's prose is. Honestly, the language in these stories really felt like it hadn't dated at all, even though most of these stories were written in the early 1930s. His descriptive language is vivid and brisk and he moves the stories along at a pace most modern readers will enjoy.<br /><br />I was also surprised at how dark and weird some of these stories are. Some of the stuff going on in them must have seemed shocking and probably blasphemous at the time. As much as people like to call the Conan stuff "sword and sorcery" they seem to overlook the strong horror element to these stories. A lot of them involve bizarre and nasty beings from the cosmic darkness and I was pleasantly unsurprised to learn that Howard and H.P. Lovecraft were buddies and IIRC Howard set most of his Conan stories in the Lovecraft universe or some variation of it. Still, they usually end with Conan drinking a giant vat of testosterone and then cutting the bad guy's head from his shoulders so I can see where people get that stuff from. I especially liked the story where Conan cuts this jellied Lovecraftian monstrosity to ribbons, as I'm concurrently reading a Lovecraft collection and most of the protagonists just unceremoniously go brain-dead or insane upon dealing with these beings.<br /><br />That said, these stories really didn't feel like an endless variation on the same simple theme; some of them deal with conspiracies, politics, mysteries, war and other fun stuff. In some of them our protagonist is a king, in some he's a thief, and sometimes even a cold-blooded murderer. He's a little more complex than most people give him credit for, Conan is, and although I don't know if I'd call him particularly deep I ended up liking him and seeing him as more than a "Me Kill Bad Man"-type figure. Instead of being annoyed at him escaping from death for the hundredth time, I enjoyed it; I mean, there's a story in here where Conan provides a deus ex machina of a sort in the form of a chair he pulls out of nowhere and throws at the bad guy's head, killing him. A chair. With any other writer, you'd probably be pissed at that ending, but with Howard it's just funny and cool.<br /><br />I learned a pleasant lesson from this collection; I shouldn't have been sitting up on my post-Arnold <i>Conan</i> movies, 21st-century throne and buying poor Mr. Howard's book with a "Provide me with cheap thrills, guy on the back cover who looks like Al Capone" mentality. There's a reason these stories are still in regular print and drawing new readers like myself, despite how marginalized they probably are by the literary-folk. Their short length makes them easy to get into and out of as well. Some of my favorites:<br /><br /><i>The Phoenix on the Sword</i>: Some dicks try to conspire to assassinate King Conan.<br /><br /><i>The Tower of the Elephant</i>: Conan attempts to steal a gem and gets way, way more than he bargained for.<br /><br /><i>The Scarlet Citadel</i>: King Conan is dethroned, imprisoned, and rises back to glory on the backs of thousands of dead men.<br /><br /><i>Iron Shadows in the Moon</i>: Conan hangs out on a creepy island and deals with pirates. I had to note the female protagonist in this story, Olivia; Howard wrote her as a recognizably human character and not just as a love/lust interest for Conan.<br /><br />Really though, each and every one of these stories is a lot of fun. All of this praise out of the way, however, it must be noted that Howard occasionally lapses into annoying early-20th-century attitudes about sex and race and shit like that, which is probably to be expected. It's not prevalent, though; take it with a grain of salt and you'll be fine. Thankfully it doesn't really damage the experience. I'm sure I'll seek out some more Howard stuff at some point in the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10668857066776781265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1278547692" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1278547692" />
    <a title="Toviel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24195415-toviel"><img alt="Toviel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548101965p2/24195415.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1278547692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8624927776666908395"><b>UPDATE</b> (1/25/2018): Rewritten for clarity. <br/><br/>The early Conan stories contain some of the best material of the pulp era. With Howard’s superb mixing of myth and history, the original Conan stories probably had more influence on <i>Dungeons and Dragons</i> than <i>Lord of the Rings</i> did!<br/><br/>The Coming of Conan the Cimmerian is an excellent jumping-on point for new fans. Aside from two stories, the book focuses on the classic character's early years as a thief, mercenary, and pirate in the mythic-esque Hyborian Age</span>
  <span id="freeText8624927776666908395" style="display:none"><b>UPDATE</b> (1/25/2018): Rewritten for clarity. <br /><br />The early Conan stories contain some of the best material of the pulp era. With Howard’s superb mixing of myth and history, the original Conan stories probably had more influence on <i>Dungeons and Dragons</i> than <i>Lord of the Rings</i> did!<br /><br />The Coming of Conan the Cimmerian is an excellent jumping-on point for new fans. Aside from two stories, the book focuses on the classic character's early years as a thief, mercenary, and pirate in the mythic-esque Hyborian Age. In addition to Conan's exciting adventures, this book also includes rough drafts, unfinished tales, extensive notes on the Conan publication history, and gorgeous artwork from Mark Schultz.<br /><br />Some of the stories come across as exceptionally racist and sexist to modern sensibilities. Furthermore, the last handful of stories become rather formulaic–while not as bad as some later Conan writers, the drop in quality is noticeable. <br /><br />Nonetheless, Howard wrote some of the best fantasy of the early twentieth century at his prime, and THE COMING OF CONAN is a must-have for any Conan fan or newcomer. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8624927776666908395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('m+w7lhciHW+c7Cf4Z76/WTEAf88rQ4WW9R08x12kTeSrQFFe4tivXIgYFNeWxWIZyFeyDkixQR8hRsRKP8nPeQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('c5dY/IpC91yXmv+FHdXgDYoL1msmI90arfm568dmE3tDOzI0f7hFb4NuzKrsrj1Nc1wbqkXRGZN5okFmpQuR5g==')})
  }

Author Michael Moorcock, already a major player in science fiction, cemented his position in the fantasy pantheon with the five-book Elric saga, of which Elric of Melniboné is the first installment. The book\'s namesake, the brooding albino emperor of the dying nation of Melniboné, is a sort of Superman for Goths, truly an archetype of the genre.\n\nThe youthful Elric is a cynical and melancholy king, heir to a nation whose 100,000-year rule of the world ended less than 500 years hence. More interested in brooding contemplation than holding the throne, Elric is a reluctant ruler, but he also realizes that no other worthy successor exists and the survival of his once-powerful, decadent nation depends on him alone. Elric\'s nefarious, brutish cousin Yrkoon has no patience for his physically weak kinsman, and he plots constantly to seize Elric\'s throne, usually over his dead body. Elric of Melniboné follows Yrkoon\'s scheming, reaching its climax in a battle between Elric and Yrkoon with the demonic runeblades Stormbringer and Mournblade. In this battle, Elric gains control of the soul-stealing Stormbringer, an event that proves pivotal to the Elric saga. --Paul Hughes<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11080096411598856747\" href=\"#\" Moore<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.76 avg rating &mdash; 1,083 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1934\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer16734140674796001400\">C. L. Moore created Jirel, ruler of Joiry, in reaction to the beefy total-testosterone blood-and-thunder tales of \'30s pulp magazines, but Jirel is no anti-Conan. She\'s a good Catholic girl, stubbornly purposeful, relentless in pursuit of enemies or <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16734140674796001400\" style=\"display:none\">C. L. Moore created Jirel, ruler of Joiry, in reaction to the beefy total-testosterone blood-and-thunder tales of \'30s pulp magazines, but Jirel is no anti-Conan. She\'s a good Catholic girl, stubbornly purposeful, relentless in pursuit of enemies or vengeance, hard-boiled and a little stupid, and cannot be distracted by mere physical attractiveness. Indeed, in Jirel\'s world, beauty = decadence = corruption. Were these stories written today, inevitably Jirel would have a lot of hot sex, but as they were first published in Weird Tales between 1934-1939, sexual attraction is mostly only vividly implied. No loss. Jirel\'s journeys through unnatural landscapes and her battles with supernatural opponents are still wonderful to read, and though newcomers Red Sonja and Xena are more famous now, Jirel rules as the       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/66700.Robert_E_Howard">About Robert E. Howard</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13430531604276897016">Robert Ervin Howard was an American pulp writer of fantasy, horror, historical adventure, boxing, western, and detective fiction. Howard wrote "over three-hundred stories and seven-hundred poems of raw power and unbridled emotion" and is especially noted for his memorable depictions of "a sombre universe of swashbuckling adventure and darkling horror."<br/><br/>He is well known for having created — in the p</span>
  <span id="freeText13430531604276897016" style="display:none">Robert Ervin Howard was an American pulp writer of fantasy, horror, historical adventure, boxing, western, and detective fiction. Howard wrote "over three-hundred stories and seven-hundred poems of raw power and unbridled emotion" and is especially noted for his memorable depictions of "a sombre universe of swashbuckling adventure and darkling horror."<br /><br />He is well known for having created — in the pages of the legendary Depression-era pulp magazine Weird Tales — the character Conan the Cimmerian, a.k.a. Conan the Barbarian, a literary icon whose pop-culture imprint can only be compared to such icons as Tarzan of the Apes, Count Dracula, Sherlock Holmes, and James Bond.<br /><br />-Wikipedia<br /><br /><i><b>Librarian Note:</b> There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13430531604276897016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
